reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Pre-norm 1D dilated convolution block: LayerNorm, GELU activation, dilated Conv1d, and residual addition.
    Common in genomic sequence models (Enformer, Basenji) for capturing long-range dependencies in DNA/RNA sequences.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels, seq_len, kernel_size, dilation):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])
        self.activation = nn.GELU()
        self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation)

    def forward(self, x):
        residual = x
        x = self.norm(x)
        x = self.activation(x)
        x = self.conv(x)
        x = x + residual
        return x

batch_size = 32
channels = 256
seq_len = 4096
kernel_size = 3
dilation = 4

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, seq_len, kernel_size, dilation]

reasoning
The task is to replace the forward computation of a specific PyTorch model with a custom CUDA kernel. The model is a pre-norm 1D dilated convolution block. Let's analyze the reference model: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, channels, seq_len, kernel_size, dilation): super(Model, self).__init__() self.norm = nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len]) self.activation = nn.GELU() self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation) def forward(self, x): residual = x x = self.norm(x) x = self.activation(x) x = self.conv(x) x = x + residual return x ``` Key components: 1. **Input `x`**: Shape `(batch_size, channels, seq_len)`. 2. **LayerNorm**: `nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])`. This normalizes over the last two dimensions (channels and seq_len) for each batch item. It computes mean and variance across `channels * seq_len` elements. * Mean: `mean = x.mean(dim=(-2, -1), keepdim=True)` * Var: `var = x.var(dim=(-2, -1), keepdim=True, unbiased=False)` * Std: `std = sqrt(var + eps)` * Normalized: `(x - mean) / std` * Scaled/Shifte: `gamma * normalized + beta` * `gamma` (weight) and `beta` (bias) have shape `(channels, seq_len)`. 3. **GELU**: `nn.GELU()`. `gelu(x) = x * 0.5 * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))`. In PyTorch, the approximation is often used: `0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))`. 4. **Conv1d**: `nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation)`. * Input: `(batch_size, channels, seq_len)`. * Weight: `(channels, channels, kernel_size)`. * Bias: `(channels,)`. * Padding = `dilation`. For `kernel_size=3`, `dilation=4`, effective kernel span is `1 + (3-1)*4 = 9`. Padding=4 on each side. * Output length of Conv1d with `padding=dilation` and `stride=1`: `L_out = floor((L_in + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size - 1) - 1) / stride) + 1` `L_out = floor((seq_len + 2*dilation - dilation*(kernel_size - 1) - 1) / 1) + 1` `L_out = seq_len + 2*dilation - dilation*kernel_size + dilation - 1 + 1` `L_out = seq_len + 3*dilation - dilation*kernel_size` With `kernel_size=3`, `L_out = seq_len + 3*dilation - 3*dilation = seq_len`. So output length is `seq_len`. * Wait, standard PyTorch Conv1d formula: $L_{out} = \lfloor \frac{L_{in} + 2 \times \text{padding} - \text{dilation} \times (\text{kernel\_size} - 1) - 1}{\text{stride}} + 1 \rfloor$. $L_{out} = \lfloor \frac{4096 + 2 \times 4 - 4 \times (3-1) - 1}{1} + 1 \rfloor = 4096 + 8 - 8 - 1 + 1 = 4096$. Yes, output length is `seq_len`. 5. **Residual Addition**: `x = x + residual`. This adds the original input `(B, C, L)` to the conv output `(B, C, L)`. Optimization Strategy: We need to fuse as many operations as possible into a single CUDA kernel to minimize memory bandwidth. The operations are: 1. Read `x` (residual). 2. Compute LayerNorm statistics over `C*L` per batch. 3. Apply LayerNorm, GELU. 4. Apply Dilated Conv1d. 5. Add residual. 6. Write result. Fusing everything into one kernel is possible but complex, especially the reduction for LayerNorm and the gemv-like convolution. A good approach might be: * **Kernel 1**: Fused LayerNorm + GELU. Writes intermediate activations to global memory (or shared memory if we tile properly). * **Kernel 2**: Fused Conv1d + Residual Add. However, the main bottleneck is likely memory bandwidth and the fact that PyTorch's Conv1d is highly optimized (cuDNN). To beat it, we need to fuse more operations. But fusing Conv1d from scratch in CUDA is hard to beat cuDNN unless we can fuse a lot of surrounding pointwise ops and the conv is small or has specific structure. Let's look at the parameters: * `batch_size = 32` * `channels = 256` * `seq_len = 4096` * `kernel_size = 3` * `dilation = 4` The convolution is $C_{out}=256, C_{in}=256, K=3$. This is a small GEMM-like operation per output position. $256 \times 256 \times 3 = 196,608$ multiply-adds per output position. Total output elements: $32 \times 256 \times 4096 \approx 33.5$M. Total FLOPs: $33.5M \times 256 \times 3 \times 2 \approx 51.5$ GFLOPs. (Wait, $256 \times 256 \times 3 \times 2$ is the FLOPs per output element? No, it's $C_{in} \times K \times 2$ MACs per output channel per position. Total MACs = $B \times C_{out} \times L_{out} \times C_{in} \times K = 32 \times 256 \times 4096 \times 256 \times 3 \approx 25.7$ GMACs $\approx 51.5$ GFLOPs). Compute: For FP32 on a modern GPU, say >10 TFLOPS. So compute time is ~5 microseconds. Memory bandwidth is likely the bottleneck. Memory traffic for reference (naive): 1. Read x (B, C, L) = 33.5M elements ~ 134 MB (fp32). 2. Write norm_x = 134 MB. Read x again for norm. Total ~268 MB. 3. Read norm_x, write gelu_x = 134 MB each. Total ~268 MB. 4. Read gelu_x, write conv_x = 134 MB + 134 MB. But conv also reads weights (256*256*3 ~ 196k elements ~ 0.8 MB) and bias. 5. Read conv_x and x, write result = 134 + 134 + 134 = 402 MB. Total memory moved (very rough): > 1 GB. At 1 TB/s, this is ~1 ms. Fused Kernel approach: If we fuse LayerNorm -> GELU -> Conv -> ResAdd into one kernel: We read x once (134 MB). We read weights and bias (~1 MB). We write result once (134 MB). Total memory ~270 MB. And we do 51.5 GFLOPs. If we can achieve this, we should be significantly faster. However, fusing a full conv (which requires $C_{in} \times K$ inputs per output channel per position) into a single kernel means we need to load a lot of input data into shared memory or registers. For a given output position $t$, the conv needs input from channels $c$ at positions $t, t-d, t+d$ (for K=3). The output at $(b, c_{out}, t)$ is $\sum_{c_{in}} \sum_{k=0..2} W[c_{out}, c_{in}, k] \cdot \text{gelu_norm}(x[b, c_{in}, t + (k-1)*d])$. To compute this efficiently, we can tile over `channels` and `seq_len`. Tile size for `seq_len` could be, say, 64 or 128. For `C_out` and `C_in`, we can tile by 32 or 64. This is essentially a GEMM with a Toeplitz/Dilated pattern. For K=3, it's sparse in the spatial dimension but dense in the channel dimension. Let's consider a single kernel that processes a batch item. For each batch $b$: Compute LayerNorm mean and variance over $C \times L$. This is a reduction. Then compute GELU for all elements. Then compute Conv. Then add residual. Reduction for LayerNorm: For $C=256, L=4096$, total elements = 1,048,576 per batch. This is a large reduction. We can do a two-stage reduction or a warp-reduce loop. Mean and Var can be computed in one pass using Welford's algorithm or two passes (mean then M2). Since we need precision, fp32 is fine. A block can process all elements. 1M elements / 1024 threads = 1024 items per thread. A grid of 1 block per batch item is fine. This block computes mean and var, stores them in shared memory. Then it proceeds to compute the normalized values and GELU. Then it computes the conv. Wait, can the same block that computes LayerNorm also compute the conv for the whole tensor? The conv needs the entire normalized+gelu'd tensor? No, to compute output at position $t$, it needs input at $t, t-d, t+d$. If a tile of threads computes output positions $T_{start} .. T_{end}$, it needs input positions roughly $T_{start}-d$ to $T_{end}+d$. Since $d=4$ and $K=3$, the halo is only 4. So we can tile the sequence dimension. For example, a block could compute a tile of $T=64$ or $128$ output positions. It needs to load a tile of $T+2d = 72$ or $136$ input positions into shared memory. But the input is $C \times L$. We also need to tile over channels. If we load all 256 channels into registers/shared memory for a spatial tile: $256 \times 136 \times 4 \text{ bytes} \approx 139 \text{ KB}$. This fits in shared memory (up to ~100KB on A100 per SM, 164KB on H100). Actually, we need to be careful. 139KB is close to the limit on some GPUs (e.g., 96KB or 164KB). We might want smaller tiles. Let's check GPU specs first.

Let me be more careful. With padding=4, each side gets 4 zeros. So the padded sequence is: `padded[l] = 0 if l < 4 else input[l-4] if l < L+4 else 0` For dilated conv, the kernel positions are at offsets 0, 4, 8 from some base. And the output at position l corresponds to starting at l in the padded input: `out[l] = sum_k weight[k] * padded[l + k * dilation]` With k in {0, 1, 2} and dilation=4: `out[l] = w[0] * padded[l] + w[1] * padded[l+4] + w[2] * padded[l+8]` Now padded[l] = input[l-4] for l in [4, L+4) So: - padded[l] = input[l-4] (or 0 if l < 4) - padded[l+4] = input[l] (valid for l in [0, L) since l+4 in [4, L+4)) - padded[l+8] = input[l+4] (valid for l in [0, L-4), and 0 for l >= L-4 since l+8 >= L+4) So for output position l: - Access input indices: l-4, l, l+4 (with out-of-bounds as 0) - For l in [0, L): l-4 is negative for l < 4, so it's 0. l+4 is valid for l < L-4. For a fixed (b, l), the computation across c_out is essentially a matrix multiplication where the "input" is the concatenation of act(norm(x[b, :, l-4])), act(norm(x[b, :, l])), act(norm(x[b, :, l+4])) and the weight is [C_out, 3*C_in]. I think the easiest efficient strategy is: - Grid: B × (C_out / C_out_tile) × (L / L_tile) - Each block computes C_out_tile × L_tile outputs. - Thread block: maybe (C_out_tile, 32) or (32, L_elements_per_thread) Let me try a different approach. Actually, let's think about what each thread does. For output at (c_out, l), the computation is: `out = residual[c_out, l] + bias[c_out] + sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^2 W[c_out, c_in, k] * gelu(norm(x[c_in, l + offset_k]))` For efficiency, we want: 1. Coalesced memory access for loading x 2. Reuse of normalized activations across c_out 3. Actually, let me think about whether we can eliminate the separate mean/var kernel and compute it on the fly. Per batch element b, mean = sum(x[b, c, l]) / (C*L), var = sum((x - mean)^2) / (C*L). For 32 batch elements, we need 32 means and 32 vars. Each is a reduction over 1M elements. Then the main kernel reads mean[b] and var[b]. Let me write this out. I'll also precompute inv_std = rsqrt(var + eps). Kernel 1: compute_mean_var ``` __global__ void compute_mean_var(const float* x, float* mean, float* inv_std, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sum_sq = 0.0f; // Actually we need two-pass for numerical stability int total_elements = C * L; int elements_per_thread = (total_elements + num_threads - 1) / num_threads; int start = tid * elements_per_thread; int end = min(start + elements_per_thread, total_elements); // One-pass reduction for (int idx = start; idx < end; idx++) { float val = x[b * C * L + idx]; local_sum += val; } // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local_sum, offset); } __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { float block_sum = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? warp_sums[tid] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { block_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, block_sum, offset); } if (tid == 0) { mean[b] = block_sum / total_elements; } } __syncthreads(); // Second pass for variance float m = mean[b]; local_sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = start; idx < end; idx++) { float diff = x[b * C * L + idx] - m; local_sum_sq += diff * diff; } // Warp reduction for variance for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local_sum_sq, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = local_sum_sq; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { float block_var = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? warp_sums[tid] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { block_var += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, block_var, offset); } if (tid == 0) { inv_std[b] = rsqrtf(block_var / total_elements + 1e-5f); } } } ``` Actually, two-pass is numerically stable but does two passes over memory. For 1M elements, that's 8MB read. With 32 batch elements, total 256MB. At 3.35TB/s that's ~0.08ms. Very fast. We can do a one-pass Welford algorithm to save memory bandwidth: ``` float mean_local = 0.0f; float M2 = 0.0f; int count = 0; for (int idx = start; idx < end; idx++) { float val = x[b * C * L + idx]; count++; float delta = val - mean_local; mean_local += delta / count; float delta2 = val - mean_local; M2 += delta * delta2; } ``` Then reduce mean_local and M2 across threads. But Welford reduction is a bit tricky since the means differ. We can use Chan's parallel algorithm or just do a standard parallel reduction. Actually, for simplicity, let's just do two-pass since the reference uses fp32 and exact LayerNorm. Two-pass over 1M elements per block is fine. Wait, actually PyTorch's LayerNorm implementation might use Welford for numerical stability. But with our two-pass, the difference from Welford should be within tolerance for fp32 (since the tolerance is 1e-4). Let me use two-pass for simplicity. Or better, use Welford to match potential reference behavior more closely. Actually, the reference does: ```python self.norm = nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len]) ``` PyTorch LayerNorm uses Welford's online algorithm in its CUDA implementation. So to match exactly, I should use Welford too... or actually, with fp32 tolerance of 1e-4, two-pass vs Welford on 1M elements should usually agree within 1e-4. But let me not take chances. Let me implement Welford: For merging two Welford states (mean_a, M2_a, count_a) and (mean_b, M2_b, count_b): ``` delta = mean_b - mean_a count = count_a + count_b mean = mean_a + delta * count_b / count M2 = M2_a + M2_b + delta * delta * count_a * count_b / count ``` So for warp reduction: ``` // lane 0 collects from all lanes float mean = local_mean; float m2 = local_M2; int cnt = local_count; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float other_mean = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, mean, offset); float other_m2 = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, m2, offset); int other_cnt = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, cnt, offset); if (other_cnt > 0) { float delta = other_mean - mean; int new_cnt = cnt + other_cnt; mean = mean + delta * other_cnt / new_cnt; m2 = m2 + other_m2 + delta * delta * cnt * other_cnt / new_cnt; cnt = new_cnt; } } ``` And similar for block reduction using shared memory. Kernel 1: ``` __global__ void compute_stats(const float* x, float* mean, float* inv_std, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; int total_elements = C * L; int elements_per_thread = (total_elements + num_threads - 1) / num_threads; int start = tid * elements_per_thread; int end = min(start + elements_per_thread, total_elements); float local_mean = 0.0f; float local_m2 = 0.0f; int local_count = 0; for (int idx = start; idx < end; idx++) { float val = x[b * C * L + idx]; local_count++; float delta = val - local_mean; local_mean += delta / local_count; float delta2 = val - local_mean; local_m2 += delta * delta2; } // Warp-level reduction for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float other_mean = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_mean, offset); float other_m2 = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_m2, offset); int other_count = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_count, offset); if (other_count > 0) { float delta = other_mean - local_mean; int new_count = local_count + other_count; local_mean = local_mean + delta * other_count / new_count; local_m2 = local_m2 + other_m2 + delta * delta * local_count * other_count / new_count; local_count = new_count; } } __shared__ float s_mean[32]; __shared__ float s_m2[32]; __shared__ int s_count[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; if (lane == 0) { s_mean[warp_id] = local_mean; s_m2[warp_id] = local_m2; s_count[warp_id] = local_count; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { local_mean = (tid < (num_threads + 31) / 32) ? s_mean[tid] : 0.0f; local_m2 = (tid < (num_threads + 31) / 32) ? s_m2[tid] : 0.0f; local_count = (tid < (num_threads + 31) / 32) ? s_count[tid] : 0; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float other_mean = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_mean, offset); float other_m2 = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_m2, offset); int other_count = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_count, offset); if (other_count > 0) { float delta = other_mean - local_mean; int new_count = local_count + other_count; local_mean = local_mean + delta * other_count / new_count; local_m2 = local_m2 + other_m2 + delta * delta * local_count * other_count / new_count; local_count = new_count; } } if (tid == 0) { mean[b] = local_mean; inv_std[b] = rsqrtf(local_m2 / total_elements + 1e-5f); } } } ``` Wait, M2 should be divided by total_elements for the biased variance. PyTorch LayerNorm uses biased variance (divides by N, not N-1). Let me verify this. Actually, PyTorch's LayerNorm computes: `std = sqrt(M2 / N + eps)` where M2 is the sum of squared differences. Yes, PyTorch uses the population standard deviation in LayerNorm. So `var = M2 / N`. Now for the main kernel. Kernel 2: ``` __global__ void fused_forward( const float* x, const float* mean, const float* inv_std, const float* weight_ln, // [C, L] const float* bias_ln, // [C, L] const float* weight_conv,// [C_out, C_in, 3] const float* bias_conv, // [C_out] float* out, int B, int C, int L ) { const int c_out_tile = 8; const int l_tile = 32; int b = blockIdx.x; int co_block = blockIdx.y; int l_block = blockIdx.z; int i = threadIdx.x / l_tile; // c_out index within tile: 0..7 int j = threadIdx.x % l_tile; // l index within tile: 0..31 int c_out = co_block * c_out_tile + i; int l_out = l_block * l_tile + j; __shared__ float smem[40]; // l_base-4 to l_base+35 float m = mean[b]; float iv = inv_std[b]; float accum = 0.0f; int l_base = l_block * l_tile; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; c_in++) { // Load x for this c_in into smem int global_l_start = l_base - 4; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 40; idx += blockDim.x) { int global_l = global_l_start + idx; if (global_l >= 0 && global_l < L) { smem[idx] = x[b * C * L + c_in * L + global_l]; } else { smem[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float x_left = smem[j]; float x_mid = smem[j + 4]; float x_right = smem[j + 8]; // LayerNorm scale and shift float gamma_left = weight_ln[c_in * L + l_out - 4]; float gamma_mid = weight_ln[c_in * L + l_out]; float gamma_right = weight_ln[c_in * L + l_out + 4]; float beta_left = bias_ln[c_in * L + l_out - 4]; float beta_mid = bias_ln[c_in * L + l_out]; float beta_right = bias_ln[c_in * L + l_out + 4]; // Normalize, scale, shift, activate x_left = ((x_left - m) * iv) * gamma_left + beta_left; x_left = 0.5f * x_left * (1.0f + erff(x_left * 0.7071067811865476f)); x_mid = ((x_mid - m) * iv) * gamma_mid + beta_mid; x_mid = 0.5f * x_mid * (1.0f + erff(x_mid * 0.7071067811865476f)); x_right = ((x_right - m) * iv) * gamma_right + beta_right; x_right = 0.5f * x_right * (1.0f + erff(x_right * 0.7071067811865476f)); // Conv weights float w0 = weight_conv[c_out * C * 3 + c_in * 3 + 0]; Let me write the code more carefully: ``` __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ inv_std, const float* __restrict__ ln_weight, const float* __restrict__ ln_bias, const float* __restrict__ conv_weight, const float* __restrict__ conv_bias, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int L ) { const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int L_TILE = 32; int b = blockIdx.x; int co_block = blockIdx.y; int l_block = blockIdx.z; int i = threadIdx.x / L_TILE; int j = threadIdx.x % L_TILE; int c_out = co_block * C_OUT_TILE + i; int l_out = l_block * L_TILE + j; __shared__ float smem[40]; float m = mean[b]; float iv = inv_std[b]; float accum = 0.0f; int l_base = l_block * L_TILE; int global_l_start = l_base - 4; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; c_in++) { // Load x for this c_in into shared memory int x_offset = b * C * L + c_in * L + global_l_start; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 40; idx += blockDim.x) { int global_l = global_l_start + idx; smem[idx] = (global_l >= 0 && global_l < L) ? callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = \"\"\"\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <cmath>\n\n#define C_OUT_TILE 8\n#define L_TILE 32\n\n__global__ void compute_stats_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ x,\n float* __restrict__ mean,\n float* __restrict__ inv_std,\n int B, int C, int L\n) {\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int num_threads = blockDim.x;\n int total_elements = C * L;\n \n float local_mean = 0.0f;\n float local_m2 = 0.0f;\n int local_count = 0;\n \n const float* x_batch = x + b * total_elements;\n \n for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elements; idx += num_threads) {\n float val = x_batch[idx];\n local_count++;\n float delta = val - local_mean;\n local_mean += delta / local_count;\n float delta2 = val - local_mean;\n local_m2 += delta * delta2;\n }\n \n // Warp-level reduction\n for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) {\n float other_mean = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_mean, offset);\n float other_m2 = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_m2, offset);\n int other_count = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_count, offset);\n \n if (other_count > 0) {\n float delta = other_mean - local_mean;\n int new_count = local_count + other_count;\n local_mean = local_mean + delta * other_count / new_count;\n local_m2 = local_m2 + other_m2 + delta * delta * local_count * other_count / new_count;\n local_count = new_count;\n }\n }\n \n __shared__ float s_mean[32];\n __shared__ float s_m2[32];\n __shared__ int s_count[32];\n \n int lane = tid % 32;\n int warp_id = tid / 32;\n \n if (lane == 0) {\n s_mean[warp_id] = local_mean;\n s_m2[warp_id] = local_m2;\n s_count[warp_id] = local_count;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n if (tid < 32) {\n local_mean = (tid < (num_threads + 31) / 32) ? s_mean[tid] : 0.0f;\n local_m2 = (tid < (num_threads + 31) / 32) ? s_m2[tid] : 0.0f;\n local_count = (tid < (num_threads + 31) / 32) ? s_count[tid] : 0;\n \n for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) {\n float other_mean = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_mean, offset);\n float other_m2 = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_m2, offset);\n int other_count = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, local_count, offset);\n \n if (other_count > 0) {\n float delta = other_mean - local_mean;\n int new_count = local_count + other_count;\n local_mean = local_mean + delta * other_count / new_count;\n local_m2 = local_m2 + other_m2 + delta * delta * local_count * other_count / new_count;\n local_count = new_count;\n }\n }\n \n if (tid == 0) {\n mean[b] = local_mean;\n inv_std[b] = rsqrtf(local_m2 / total_elements + 1e-5f);\n }\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void fused_forward_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ x,\n const float* __restrict__ mean,\n const float* __restrict__ inv_std,\n const float* __restrict__ ln_weight,\n const float* __restrict__ ln_bias,\n const float* __restrict__ conv_weight,\n const float* __restrict__ conv_bias,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n int B, int C, int L\n) {\n __shared__ float smem[L_TILE + 8];\n \n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int co_block = blockIdx.y;\n int l_block = blockIdx.z;\n \n int i = threadIdx.x / L_TILE;\n int j = threadIdx.x % L_TILE;\n \n int c_out = co_block * C_OUT_TILE + i;\n int l_out = l_block * L_TILE + j;\n \n float m = mean[b];\n float iv = inv_std[b];\n float accum = 0.0f;\n \n int l_base = l_block * L_TILE;\n int global_pad_start = l_base;\n int x_offset_batch = b * C * L;\n \n for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; c_in++) {\n int x_offset = x_offset_batch + c_in * L;\n \n for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < L_TILE + 8; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int global_pad_idx = global_pad_start + idx;\n if (global_pad_idx >= 4 && global_pad_idx < L + 4) {\n smem[idx] = x[x_offset + global_pad_idx - // Mean and inv_std for this batch float m = mean[b]; float iv = inv_std[b]; float accum = 0.0f; int l_base = l_block * L_TILE; int global_pad_start = l_base; int x_offset_batch = b * C * L; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; c_in++) { int x_offset = x_offset_batch + c_in * L; // Load shared memory for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < L_TILE + 8; idx += blockDim.x) { int global_pad_idx = global_pad_start + idx; if (global_pad_idx >= 4 && global_pad_idx < L + 4) { smem[idx] = x[x_offset + global_pad_idx - 4]; v0 = 0.5f * v0 * (1.0f + erff(v0 * 0.7071067811865476f)); } xk = smem[j + 4]; float v1 = (xk - m) * iv; v1 = v1 * ln_weight[c_in * L + l_out] + ln_bias[c_in * L + l_out]; v1 = 0.5f * v1 * (1.0f + erff(v1 * 0.7071067811865476f)); xk = smem[j + 8]; float v2 = 0.0f; if (l_out < L - 4) { // padded[l_out + 8] = x[l_out + 4] v2 = (xk - m) * iv; v2 = v2 * ln_weight[c_in * L + l_out + 4] + ln_bias[c_in * L + l_out + 4]; v2 = 0.5f * v2 * (1.0f + erff(v2 * 0.7071067811865476f)); __syncthreads(); } float residual = x[x_offset_batch + c_out * L + l_out]; float bias = conv_bias[c_out]; out[x_offset_batch + c_out * L + l_out] = residual + bias + accum; ``` Wait, I have a bug! When `l_out >= 4`, `v0` accesses `ln_weight[c_in * L + l_out - 4]`. But `l_out - 4 = l_base + j - 4`. This is valid when `l_base + j >= 4`, which is guaranteed by `l_out >= 4`. Good. For `v2`, when `l_out < L - 4`, `v2` accesses `ln_weight[c_in * L + l_out + 4]`. Here `l_out + 4 = l_base + j + 4`. This needs to be < L, which is guaranteed by `l_out < L - 4`. Good. So the indexing is correct. Now, let me think about the numerical mismatch. The differences are small - could they be due to floating point associativity differences in the accumulation? In the reference, PyTorch uses a highly optimized GEMM for the convolution (im2col + cublas). In my code, I accumulate in a different order. For the convolution, the reference computes: ``` out[c_out, l] = sum(c_in, k) weight[c_out, c_in, k] * gelu(norm(x[c_in, l + k*dilation - padding])) ``` The exact order of accumulation for the inner product over `c_in` and `k` might differ. PyTorch uses a matrix multiplication which might reorder the additions differently. But the tolerance is `1e-4` for fp32. Are accumulation order differences typically that large? For `C=256`, `kernel_size=3`, we're summing `256 * 3 = 768` terms. If each term has an error of ~1e-7 (machine epsilon for float32), the total rounding error from different accumulation orders could be on the order of `sqrt(768) * 1e-7 ≈ 2.8e-6` (random walk model) or `768 * 1e-7 = 7.7e-5` (worst case). This matches the observed error of ~7e-4? Hmm, not quite. Actually, the terms are not just rounding errors; the actual values being accumulated can be significant. Actually, the difference bound for different summation orders is roughly the number of terms times machine epsilon times the magnitude of the sum. If the sum has magnitude ~1, and there are 768 terms, the difference could be up to `768 * 1e-7 * 1 = 7.7e-5`. But the observed max difference is ~7e-4, which is about 10x larger. reasoning
The error is still persistent. Let me think about what else could cause a mismatch. Looking at the conv1d in the reference: - nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=4, dilation=4) The Conv1d expects input of shape [B, C, L]. Since the input x after norm/activation is [B, C, L] and the convolution is normal 1D temporal convolution, the weights shape should be [out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_size]. Since the default groups=1 and both in and out channels are 256, the weight shape is [256, 256, 3]. In our inference with torch-gradcheck, when we load_ref_weights, the conv.weight would have shape [256, 256, 3]. But in our reference model: ```python self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation) ``` With `kernel_size=3`, `dilation=4`, the weight shape is indeed [256, 256, 3]. BUT: Looking at the kernel registration, I load weights from `self.conv.weight`. Since the inference does `inst_new = ModelNew(*init_inputs)` and then loads_ref_weights, and the reference model is loaded using `Model(*init_inputs)`, which sets up the Conv1d with `kernel_size=3, dilation=4`, the weight shape should match. In our kernel, the weight indexing `int w_base = c_out * C * 3 + c_in * 3;` assumes weight shape [C_out, C_in, 3]. For Conv1d, the weight shape is [out_channels, in_channels / groups, kernel_size]. It is stored as [256, 256, 3], but the layout might be [out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size] which in terms of memory is just contiguous. Since `C_out * C_in * kernel_size`, and `stride = (C_in*kernel_size, kernel_size, 1)`, our indexing should be right. Wait - is there a possibility that the Conv1d is cross-correlation rather than convolution? In PyTorch, Conv1d uses cross-correlation, which is equivalent to convolution with flipped kernel. But our kernel uses the weights directly (multiplies the current index with the neighbor values). This is consistent with Correlation, so we shouldn't need to flip. Could there be an issue with memory coalescing or out-of-bounds access (using shared memory for pad=4, but indexing `global_pad_idx` by `blockIdx.z * L_TILE`). In the current kernel, `global_pad_start = l_base = l_block * L_TILE`. Since `l_out = l_block * L_TILE + j`, `global_pad_idx = l_base + idx`, so `smem[j]` accesses `x[x_offset + l_base + j - 4]` = `x[x_offset + l_out - 4]`, which is correct. But when `l_out >= 4`, we need `l_out-4` index, which for `l_out` in `[4, L-1]` maps to `smem[j]`. For `l_out = 4`, `j=4`, `smem[4] = x[x_offset + l_base + 0] = x[x_offset]` correct. Could there be a problem with floating point accumulation order causing slight differences? In our kernel, we accumulate as `accum += w0 * v0 + w1 * v1 + w2 * v2;` iterating over c_in. PyTorch performs matmul or im2col conv, which accumulates in a different order. This could cause tiny differences. But the difference seems like ~7e-4, which could be just from differing operation orders. The tolerance for fp32 is `atol=rtol=1e-4`. Our max diff is ~7e-4, so it's exceeding tolerance. Could the difference be because PyTorch uses a more accurate GELU? Looking at the PyTorch GELU implementation. In PyTorch, the GELU forward uses: ```python 0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2))) ``` with an approximation `tanh` for faster compute by default when your PyTorch version uses the approximate flag. In the reference model, since it doesn't specify `approximate`, the default is `'none'` in recent versions (since PyTorch 1.12), using the exact formula with `erf`. The CUDA `erff` function should give very similar results to the CPU/PyTorch `erf`, but could differ slightly. Is that causing ~7e-4 error? Probably not that large. Another thought: Could there be an issue with LayerNorm affine? The `nn.LayerNorm` uses weight and bias of shape `[channels, seq_len]`. Could there be numerical instability in `(x_batch[idx] - mean) * inv_std` where we use `mean[b]` as a batch-wide mean? The reference LayerNorm uses the exact same formula. So that should match. Wait, one more thing: the Conv1d in PyTorch uses a specific algorithm (e.g., CuDNN) which might implement cross-correlation directly. But padding=4, dilation=4, kernel_size=3. For an output position `l_out` in `[0, L-1]`, the input positions sampled are: - `l_in[0] = l_out + padding - dilation * (kernel_size - 1) / something?` Actually, for Conv1d, the output formula is: ``` out[l] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[c_out, c_in, k] * input[c_in, l + pad - k*dilation] ``` So the leftmost (spatially) input index accessed is `l + pad - (K-1)*dilation` = `l + 4 - 2*4` = `l - 4`. The rightmost is `l + pad - 0` = `l + 4`. Wait, that's shifted! Ah! This is the standard formula. For `padding=4`, the output at position `l` samples input at `l + 4 - k*dilation` for k=0,1,2. - k=0: `l + 4` (rightmost) - k=1: `l + 4 - 4 = l` (middle) - k=2: `l + 4 - 8 = l - 4` (leftmost) So the kernel order in the weight array is: - weight[*,*,0] is applied to `l + 4` (rightmost/future) - weight[*,*,1] is applied to `l` - weight[*,*,2] is applied to `l - 4` (leftmost/past) But in our kernel, we used: - `w0` for pad_idx-4 corresponding to `l-4` - `w1` for pad_idx corresponding to `l` - `w2` for pad_idx+4 corresponding to `l+4` So we assigned `w0` to the "past" position but mapped it to `conv_weight[0]`, which in PyTorch corresponds to the "future" position `l + 4`. We've got the weights effectively reversed! To match PyTorch Conv1d, we need to use: - `w_base + 0` should multiply the "future" sample (index + 4) - `w_base + 1` should multiply the current sample (index) - `w_base + 2` should multiply the "past" sample (index - 4) This is because PyTorch correlation uses `weight[k]` for input `l + pad - k*dilation`. So larger k -> smaller input index. For k=0: input `l+4`, larger index. So our kernel should map: - `v2` (which uses `l_out + 4`) * `conv_weight[0]` - `v1` (which uses `l_out`) * `conv_weight[1]` - `v0` (which uses `l_out - 4`) * `conv_weight[2]` Let's fix this and try again! Also, I should handle the boundary conditions. PyTorch produces output of size L, not L+8. The padding is 4 on each side, but because the kernel is dilated with dilation=4, the effective padding ensures that the output has size L. Specifically, for output position `l`, input positions are `l+4`, `l`, `l-4`. For `l=0`, inputs are `4`, `0`, `-4`. The position `-4` is out of bounds but padded with 0. For `l=L-1`, inputs are `L+3`, `L-1`, `L-5`. `L+3` is padded with 0. So our existing logic handles that if we check correctly. Wait, but in PyTorch, conv1d with padding=4 on input of size L will internally pad with 4 zeros on each side (total input becomes L+8), then dilated conv is applied. Output will be `(L+8-1) / 1 + 1`... Wait, let me compute: with stride=1 (default), output_size = floor((L + 2*pad - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - 1) / stride + 1) = L + 8 - 8 - 1 + 1 = L. Good. So for position `l_out` in range [0, L-1], the padded input positions accessed (with dilation) are: - For kernel index k=0: padded position is `l_out + 4 - 0 = l_out + 4`. In original input [0, L-1], this is `l_out + 4`. For `l_out` in [0, L-1], `l_out + 4` is in [4, L+3]. Positions >= L are padded. - For kernel index k=1: padded position is `l_out + 4 - 4 = l_out`. This is always valid in [0, L-1]. - For kernel index k=2: padded position is `l_out + 4 - 8 = l_out - 4`. For `l_out` in [0, L-1], this is in [-4, L-5]. Positions < 0 are padded. So for our kernel using `smem` which stores padded input: - `smem[j]` (at `l_base + j`) = padded position `l_out`. We want to multiply this by `weight[1]`. - `smem[j-4]` = padded position `l_out - 4`. We want to multiply this by `weight[2]`. But `j-4` is not good because `j` is the thread index in the shared memory block. Actually, `smem` stores padded positions from `l_base` to `l_base + L_TILE + 7`. `l_out = l_base + j`. - `smem[j]` corresponds to padded position `l_out`? No, we stored `smem[idx] = x[x_offset + l_base + idx - 4]`. So `smem[4]` is `x[x_offset + l_base]`. In general, `smem[4 + j]` is `x[x_offset + l_base + j]`. Therefore: - `smem[j + 4]` is `x[x_offset + l_out]` -> corresponds to padded position `l_out` (multiplied by `weight[1]`) - `smem[j + 4 - 4] = smem[j]` is `x[x_offset + l_out - 4]` -> corresponds to padded position `l_out - 4` (multiplied by `weight[2]`) - `smem[j + 4 + 4] = smem[j + 8]` is `x[x_offset + l_out + 4]` -> corresponds to padded position `l_out + 4` (multiplied by `weight[0]`) So the correct mapping should be: - `w0 = conv_weight[w_base + 0]` multiplies `smem[j + 8]` (future) - `w1 = conv_weight[w_base + 1]` multiplies `smem[j + 4]` (current) - `w2 = conv_weight[w_base + 2]` multiplies `smem[j]` (past) And the boundary checks are: - For `l_out - 4 < 0` (i.e., `l_out < 4`): `smem[j]` is in the left padding area. Condition: `l_out >= 4` for `smem[j]` to be valid. - For `l_out + 4 >= L` (i.e., `l_out >= L - 4`): `smem[j + 8]` is in the right padding area. Condition: `l_out < L - 4` for `smem[j+8]` to be valid. This means our previous code had the weights for `w0` and `w2` swapped! Since we used `w0 * v0 + w1 * v1 + w2 * v2` where `v0` was `past` and `v2` was `future`, we were applying the weights in the wrong temporal order compared to PyTorch. Let's swap them: `w0` should multiply `future`, `w2` should multiply `past`. Also, check the boundary conditions more carefully. For `l_out < 4`, indices `l_out - 4` are negative, so they get padded. Our data loading loads `0` for positions outside. `smem[j]` corresponds to position `l_out - 4`. The index `j` is in `[0, L_TILE-1]`. `smem[j]` was loaded from `global_pad_idx = l_base + j = l_out`. Then it loads `x[x_offset + l_out - 4]`. For `l_out < 4`, `l_out - 4` is negative, so our loader doesn't access `x` -> `smem[j] = 0`. But we also guard `if (l_out >= 4)` for `v0`. So that's correct. For `smem[j+8]`, this corresponds to `x[x_offset + l_out + 4]`. For `l_out >= L - 4`, `l_out + 4 >= L`, the loader sets `smem[j+8]` = 0, and we guard `if (l_out < L - 4)`. So that's correct. Also, check: `if (l_out < L - 4)` should be `if (l_out + 4 < L)` -> `if (l_out < L - 4)`. Correct. Let's update the kernel with the corrected weight mapping.

Looking at the reference more carefully: ```python self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation) ``` When I access `self.conv.weight` in PyTorch, it has shape `[out_channels, in_channels / groups, kernel_size]`. For groups=1, it's `[256, 256, 3]`. The data layout is contiguous in C order, so: - `conv_weight[c_out, c_in, k]` corresponds to `conv_weight[c_out * (C * 3) + c_in * 3 + k]` In my kernel, I used: ```cpp int w_base = c_out * C * 3 + c_in * 3; float w0 = conv_weight[w_base + 0]; float w1 = conv_weight[w_base + 1]; float w2 = conv_weight[w_base + 2]; ``` This corresponds to `conv_weight[c_out, c_in, 0]`, `conv_weight[c_out, c_in, 1]`, `conv_weight[c_out, c_in, 2]`. For Conv1d with kernel_size=3, dilation=4, and padding=4: - At output position t, the conv reads input at positions [t-4, t, t+4] (because of dilation) - The kernel is indexed as k=0, 1, 2, corresponding to positions t-4, t, t+4 But which conv weight goes with which position? In PyTorch, for `Conv1d`, the weights are applied from left to right: `weight[k]` is applied to `input[t + k * dilation - padding]` for each output position t after padding. Actually, for `Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, padding, dilation)`: - The input is padded by `padding` on each side - The kernel of size `kernel_size` with dilation `dilation` has effective size `(kernel_size - 1) * dilation + 1` - For output position `t`, the kernel reads input positions: - `start = t * stride - padding + k * dilation` for k = 0, 1, ..., kernel_size-1 - With stride=1, padding=4, dilation=4: - start = t - 4 + k * 4 - For k=0: t - 4 - For k=1: t - For k=2: t + 4 So `weight[0]` is multiplied by input at `t - 4`, `weight[1]` by input at `t`, `weight[2]` by input at `t + 4`. Actually, in the standard (non-transposed) convolution, the formula is: ``` out[t] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[k] * padded_input[t + k * dilation] ``` where `padded_input` includes padding. My manual conv kernel has each thread doing 256*3 = 768 multiply-adds. This might not be the most efficient on the GPU due to poor parallelism. For a block of 256 threads (8x32 = 256), each thread processes one output. With 256 blocks per batch and 32 batches, we have 8192 blocks. Each block computes 256 output channels? No, each block computes 8 output channels x 32 length positions = 256 outputs. On H100 with 132 SMs (actually Hopper H100 has 132 or 144 SMs depending on variant), 8192 blocks is plenty of parallelism. But each block reads 256 input channels * 40 floats = 10KB into shared memory. The shared memory usage is small (40 floats = 160 bytes). So many blocks can run concurrently. The compute per block: 256 outputs * (256 input channels * 3 MACs each) = 256 * 768 = 196,608 MACs. At high clock, an H100 SM can do many FLOPS. But due to memory bandwidth (reading weights for 8 different output channels per block), the performance might be memory-bound. Actually, looking at the problem again, the conv weights for 8 output channels total 8 * 256 * 3 = 6,144 floats = 24KB. These are read from global memory once per block (by each thread). Since all threads read the same weights for a given c_in/c_out pair, but each thread handles a different c_out, threads read different weights. These reads are not coalesced! For a given c_in, thread with c_out=0 reads w[0, c_in, 0..2], thread with c_out=1 reads w[1, c_in, 0..2], etc. These are strided in memory (stride = C * 3 floats = 256 * 3 * 4 = 3072 bytes). This means each warp (32 threads) reading weights will issue 8 different cache lines (since 32 threads * 3072 bytes stride >> cache line size). That's very inefficient! In fact, this could be the main performance bottleneck. The memory access pattern for weights is terrible. Let me think about a better weight access pattern. Alternative 1: Instead of having each thread handle a different output channel, let them handle the same output channel but different length positions. Then within a warp, all threads read the same weights. But we'd need more blocks for different output channels. Alternative 2: Transpose the weights in memory. PyTorch stores weights as [C_out, C_in, K]. What if I pre-transpose them to [C_in, K, C_out]? Then threads reading for different C_out would be reading adjacent memory. Wait, in the constructor, I have access to `self.conv.weight`. I could transpose it and store it in a different format. But then during correctness check, does the weight get loaded correctly? Actually, for conv1d, PyTorch initializes `self.conv.weight` with shape [C_out, C_in / groups, K]. By default, I could transpose this and save it. For example: ```python def __init__(self, ...): super().__init__() self.conv = nn.Conv1d(...) # Transpose weight for better memory access: [C_in, K, C_out] self.conv_weight_t = self.conv.weight.permute(1, 2, 0).contiguous() ``` Then in the CUDA kernel, I access weights as `conv_weight_t[c_in * 3 * C_out + k * C_out + c_out]` which is much more coalesced when different c_out threads read together. In PyTorch: ```python gelu = 0.5 * x * (1 + torch.erf(x / math.sqrt(2))) ``` In C: ```cpp float gelu = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x / sqrtf(2.0f))); ``` Wait, I used `0.7071067811865476f` which is `1/sqrt(2)` in float. Dividing `1.0f / sqrtf(2.0f)` gives the same result: `0.70710677f`. So `x * 0.7071067811865476f` vs `x / sqrtf(2.0f)` - these should give the exact same bit pattern. Let me check: in PyTorch, `torch.erf(x)` where x is float32. The operation `x / math.sqrt(2)` is computed as follows: - In Python: `math.sqrt(2)` is Python float (double precision). `x` is a PyTorch float32 tensor. Division promotes to double? No, PyTorch follows tensor dtypes. A float32 tensor divided by a Python float gives float32 result. Actually, in PyTorch: ```python x = torch.randn(2, dtype=torch.float32) result = x / math.sqrt(2) ``` Here `math.sqrt(2)` is a Python float. PyTorch treats Python floats as scalars and follows type promotion rules. For `float32_tensor / scalar_float`, the result is float32. The scalar is cast to float32 before division. So PyTorch computes `(float32)x / (float32)1.41421356`, which is the same as `x * 0.70710677f` in float32. Then `torch.erf(result)` operates on float32 and returns float32. So in theory, `erff(x * 0.70710677f)` in CUDA should match `torch.erf(x / math.sqrt(2))` in PyTorch exactly. But in practice, different math libraries (`erff` from libm vs CUDA's device `erff` vs PyTorch's custom implementation) might give slightly different results. PyTorch might use its own implementation of `erf` on CUDA rather than the CUDA math library's `erff`. Let me check. In PyTorch's ATen CUDA code for `erf`, it likely uses `::erf` which calls the CUDA device function. But PyTorch's dispatching might do additional things. Actually, looking at PyTorch source for CUDA `erf`: ``` AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES_AND2(...) gpu_kernel(iter, []GPU_LAMBDA(scalar_t a) -> scalar_t { return ::erf(a); }); ``` This calls the CUDA standard `erf`/`erff`. So PyTorch uses the same CUDA math library. BUT, and this is important, PyTorch's `GELU` implementation is NOT just `erf`. It might use a custom kernel that handles special cases, uses FMA (fused multiply-add), or does something else. Let me check PyTorch's GELU CUDA implementation. It could be in `ActivationGeluKernel.cu`. ```cpp template <typename T> struct Gelu { T operator()(const T& x) const { constexpr T kAlpha = M_SQRT1_2; return x * T(0.5) * (T(1) + ::erf(x * kAlpha)); } }; ``` For float, this becomes: ```cpp return x * 0.5f * (1.0f + ::erff(x * 0.70710678118654752440f)); ``` Wait! The constant is `M_SQRT1_2` which is cast to `T`. For float `T`, this is `(float)M_SQRT1_2`, which has the same value as my hardcoded constant. But note the order of operations: `x * kAlpha` first, then `::erf()`, then `1 + ...`, then `x * 0.5`, then multiply. In my kernel I had: ```cpp v1 = 0.5f * v1 * (1.0f + erff(v1 * 0.7071067811865476f)); ``` This should be mathematically equivalent. But floating point operations are not associative, so `x * 0.5 * y` vs `(x * 0.5) * y` can differ by 1 ULP. Over many operations, this could add up. However, a single difference of 1 ULP in GELU out of 768 accumulated terms would cause a difference of about `1e-7 * 768 * weight_magnitude ≈ 1e-4`. That's close to our observed difference! Slightly different arithmetic ordering To minimize this, I could try to exactly match the expression: ```cpp v1 = v1 * 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(v1 * 0.7071067811865476f)); ``` Or even use `fma()` explicitly?
